vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\"><br /></span><span style=\"font-family: Arial; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\"><br /></span><span style=\"font-family: Arial; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; font-weight: 700; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">General support for Chrome Apps on Chrome OS will remain enabled, without requiring any policy setting, through June 2022.</span></p></td></tr><tr style=\"height: 0pt;\"><td style=\"border-bottom: solid #000000 1pt; border-left: solid #000000 1pt; border-right: solid #000000 1pt; border-top: solid #000000 1pt; overflow-wrap: break-word; overflow: hidden; padding: 5pt 5pt 5pt 5pt; vertical-align: top;\"><p dir=\"ltr\" style=\"line-height: 1.2; margin-bottom: 0pt; margin-top: 0pt;\"><span style=\"font-family: Arial; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">June 2022</span></p></td><td style=\"border-bottom: solid #000000 1pt; border-left: solid #000000 1pt; border-right: solid #000000 1pt; border-top: solid #000000 1pt; overflow-wrap: break-word; overflow: hidden; padding: 5pt 5pt 5pt 5pt; vertical-align: top;\"><p dir=\"ltr\" style=\"line-height: 1.2; margin-bottom: 0pt; margin-top: 0pt;\"><span style=\"font-family: Arial; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">Chrome Web Store will stop accepting new and updated </span><span style=\"font-family: Arial; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; font-weight: 700; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">private and unlisted</span><span style=\"font-family: Arial; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\"> Chrome apps.</span></p><br /><p dir=\"ltr\" style=\"line-height: 1.2; margin-bottom: 0pt; margin-top: 0pt;\"><span style=\"font-family: Arial; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">End support for Chrome Apps, NaCl, PNaCl, and PPAPI for </span><span style=\"font-family: Arial; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; font-weight: 700; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">all platforms</span><span style=\"font-family: Arial; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">.</span></p></td></tr></tbody></table></div></span></div><div><br /></div><div><br /></div><div><br /></div><div>This change does not impact support for Chrome Extensions. Google will continue to support and invest in Chrome Extensions on all existing platforms. Fostering a robust ecosystem of extensions is critical to Chrome's mission and we are committed to providing a useful extension platform for customizing the browsing experience for all users.&nbsp;</div><div><br /></div><div><br /></div><div><br /></div><div>For additional support with Chrome app migration, please visit our <a href=\"https://developers.chrome.com/apps/migration\">Chrome Apps migration site</a>. This page will be kept up to date as we progress together through this process.</div><div><br /></div><div><br /></div><div><br /></div><div>We thank our community of developers who have provided feedback to help us shape this modified and simplified approach. We are inspired by a future beyond Chrome apps, where the ecosystem continues forward progress leveraging open Web standards across all modern browsers.</div><div><br /></div><div><br /></div><div><br /></div><div><br /></div><div>Posted by Anthony Laforge, Technical Director, Chrome Platform Team</div><div><br /></div></span></div>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Mon, 10 Aug 2020 18:10:00 +0000","feedId":649,"bgimg":"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-vkF7AFJOwBk/VkQxeAGi1mI/AAAAAAAARYo/57denvsQ8zA/s1600-r/logo_chromium.png","linkMd5":"346f7f3499eb49090405fa92bb19b711","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn61@2020_5/2020/08/16/12-29-50-106_40b5b15e774afa69.webp","metaImg":"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-vkF7AFJOwBk/VkQxeAGi1mI/AAAAAAAARYo/57denvsQ8zA/s1600-r/logo_chromium.png","destWidth":533,"destHeight":537,"author":"Chromium Blog"},{"createdTime":"2020-08-16 20:29:49","updatedTime":"2020-08-16 20:29:49","title":"Using Chrome to generate more accessible PDFs","link":"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/07/using-chrome-to-generate-more.html","description":"<span id=\"docs-internal-guid-6c590ccc-7fff-0abf-e8a7-7b7cdc932e88\"><p dir=\"ltr\" style=\"line-height: 1.38; margin-bottom: 0pt; margin-top: 0pt;\"><span style=\"font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">Starting in Chrome 85 (scheduled to go to stable in late August), Chrome will automatically generate a tagged PDF when you print a web page and choose the \"Save as PDF\" destination. A tagged PDF is one that contains extra metadata about the structure of a document, including things like headings, lists, tables, paragraphs, and image descriptions. Tagged PDFs are more accessible to users with disabilities, such as blind users who use a screen reader to access PDF files. Tagged PDFs can have other uses too, like making it easier for software that needs to automatically process and extract data from PDFs.</span></p><div><span><br /></span></div><br /><p dir=\"ltr\" style=\"line-height: 1.38; margin-bottom: 0pt; margin-top: 0pt;\"><span style=\"font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">We think adding this metadata to PDFs is a perfect fit for Chrome, because that information is already available in well-structured, accessible web pages. We hope this helps make more content exported from Chrome to be accessible to even more users.</span></p><div><span><br /></span></div><br /><p dir=\"ltr\" style=\"line-height: 1.38; margin-bottom: 0pt; margin-top: 0pt;\"><span style=\"font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">Organizations that publish content for the general public online often require that all of their PDFs are accessible, either as a matter of policy or to comply with local laws such as </span><a href=\"https://www.section508.gov/\" style=\"text-decoration-line: none;\"><span style=\"color: #1155cc; font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; text-decoration-line: underline; text-decoration-skip-ink: none; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">Section 508</span></a><span style=\"font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\"> in the U.S.&nbsp; Unfortunately, a lot of software programs that are otherwise great for authoring content don't have any support for directly generating a tagged PDF. In these cases, separate remediation software or services are used to make PDFs compliant. By building this into Chrome, we're hoping some organizations that already use HTML as part of their document workflow might be able to take advantage of this new functionality and generate compliant PDFs more easily. This feature also works with </span><a href=\"https://developers.google.com/web/updates/2017/04/headless-chrome\" style=\"text-decoration-line: none;\"><span style=\"color: #1155cc; font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; text-decoration-line: underline; text-decoration-skip-ink: none; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">Chrome Headless</span></a><span style=\"font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\"> when you use both the --print-to-pdf and --export-tagged-pdf flags.</span></p><div><span><br /></span></div><br /><p dir=\"ltr\" style=\"line-height: 1.38; margin-bottom: 0pt; margin-top: 0pt;\"><span style=\"font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">When we started our journey to make PDFs more accessible, we reached out to the experts - </span><a href=\"https://commonlook.com/about/get-started-with-commonlook/?utm_source=google-accessibility-news&amp;utm_medium=other&amp;utm_campaign=google2020\" style=\"text-decoration-line: none;\"><span style=\"color: #1155cc; font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; text-decoration-line: underline; text-decoration-skip-ink: none; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">CommonLook</span></a><span style=\"font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">, an organization that's been offering PDF accessibility software and services for more than 20 years and which is active in setting PDF standards. We made use of CommonLook's PDF Validator and consulted with them to ensure we were focusing our efforts on the areas that would have the biggest impact.</span></p><div><span><br /></span></div><br /><p dir=\"ltr\" style=\"line-height: 1.38; margin-bottom: 0pt; margin-left: 36pt; margin-top: 0pt;\"><span style=\"font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-style: italic; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">\"To improve the accessibility of PDF documents in Chrome, Google reached out to CommonLook because of our expertise in PDF accessibility. At the time, we recognized the potential impact on PDF accessibility due to the massive number of Chrome users around the world. Two years later, we are pleased to announce that significant progress has been made, and now Chrome is rolling out this feature to all users every time they generate a PDF from Chrome. We will continue to support Google as they work to make Chrome more accessible to all their users.\" - Monir ElRayes, President and CEO, CommonLook</span></p><div><span><br /></span></div><br /><p dir=\"ltr\" style=\"line-height: 1.38; margin-bottom: 0pt; margin-top: 0pt;\"><span style=\"font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">This feature is rolling out as an experiment. Use chrome://flags/#export-tagged-pdf if you'd like to try it out before it's enabled automatically for all users.</span></p><div><span><br /></span></div><br /><p dir=\"ltr\" style=\"line-height: 1.38; margin-bottom: 0pt; margin-top: 0pt;\"><span style=\"font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">While this is an important milestone, we're not done. Future work includes both improving the quality of generated tagged PDFs, and also improving Chrome's built-in PDF reader to better consume tagged PDFs.</span></p><div><span><br /></span></div><div><span><br /></span></div><br /><p dir=\"ltr\" style=\"line-height: 1.38; margin-bottom: 0pt; margin-top: 0pt;\"><span style=\"font-family: &quot;Google Sans&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 11pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\">Posted by Dominic Mazzoni, technical lead for Chrome accessibility.</span></p></span>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 29 Jul 2020 16:02:00 +0000","feedId":649,"bgimg":"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-vkF7AFJOwBk/VkQxeAGi1mI/AAAAAAAARYo/57denvsQ8zA/s1600-r/logo_chromium.png","linkMd5":"bdd2fc7f05a64cc6f8188c064937b6b9","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn61@2020_5/2020/08/16/12-29-50-106_40b5b15e774afa69.webp","metaImg":"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-vkF7AFJOwBk/VkQxeAGi1mI/AAAAAAAARYo/57denvsQ8zA/s1600-r/logo_chromium.png","destWidth":533,"destHeight":537,"author":"Chromium Blog"},{"createdTime":"2020-08-16 20:29:49","updatedTime":"2020-08-16 20:29:49","title":"The Science Behind Web Vitals","link":"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html","description":"<p> <a href=\"https://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/introducing-web-vitals-essential-metrics.html\">Web Vitals</a> is an initiative by Google to help business owners, marketers and developers alike identify opportunities to improve user experiences. These signals are guided by extensive work by many researchers in the fields of human–computer interaction (HCI) and user experience (UX). But figuring out the right metrics and thresholds is not as simple as picking up a research paper and finding the answer. </p> \n<h2 style=\"font-size: 2em; line-height: 1.3em; margin: 20px 0px 10px;\">Journeys, not pages</h2> \n<p> Imagine you’re walking through an unfamiliar city to get to an important appointment. You walk through various streets and city centers on your way. But here and there, loose paving stones make you trip, there are slow automatic doors you have to wait for to open, and unexpected construction detours lead you astray. All of these events interrupt your progress, increase stress and distract you from reaching your destination. </p> \n<p> People using the web are also on a journey, with each of their actions constituting one step in what would ideally be a continuous flow. And just like in the real world, they can be interrupted by delays, distracted from their tasks and led to make errors. These events, in turn, can lead to reduced satisfaction and abandonment of a site or the whole journey. </p> \n<p> In both cases, removing interruptions and obstacles is the key to a smooth journey and a satisfied user. </p> \n<p> So what trips users up on the web? </p> \n<h2 style=\"font-size: 2em; line-height: 1.3em; margin: 20px 0px 10px;\">Interruptions due to waiting</h2> \n<p> The most common type of interruption web users experience is waiting for pages to load. For a developer, a page load is a discrete event and some delay might feel inevitable. However, a page load more often happens in the <em>middle</em> of a user's journey, as they learn about recent events in the news, research a new product or add items to a cart for purchase. So from the user's point of view, loading a particular page doesn't represent a natural break: they haven’t yet achieved their goal, which may make them less tolerant of delays.<sup style=\"font-size: x-small;\"><a href=\"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html#f1\">1</a></sup> This means pages need to load fast so the user's journey can flow smoothly. </p> \n<p> How fast is fast enough? In a way, that’s the wrong question. There’s no single magic number and there's three main reasons why. </p> \n<p> First, the answer depends on the outcome you consider, for instance abandonment, user satisfaction or task performance. Different studies focus on different outcomes and yield different results. </p> \n<p> Second, the effect of delays varies hugely depending on a user's personality, past experience and the urgency of their task.<sup style=\"font-size: x-small;\"><a href=\"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html#f2\">2</a></sup> For example, if you were to plot how many users stayed on a site as a function of the delay they experienced, you would not see a clean step from 100% to 0% after X seconds. You would instead see a smooth distribution that might look like this: </p> \n<a href=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-IXbatnoEi3o/XsbX2NvIC-I/AAAAAAAABLw/qtdYK6Zs4Jktb3TSyStLeZT7qXNtcAFOACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/users_remaining_delay.png\" style=\"margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 1em;\"> <img alt=\"Chart showing the percent of users remaining decreasing as the delay increases\" border=\"0\" data-original-height=\"209\" data-original-width=\"563\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-IXbatnoEi3o/XsbX2NvIC-I/AAAAAAAABLw/qtdYK6Zs4Jktb3TSyStLeZT7qXNtcAFOACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/users_remaining_delay.png\" title=\"\" /></a> \n<p> So we must ask: which point on this curve do we aim for? In other words, how much do we invest in speed on the one hand, and how many of our users will we lose on the other? </p> \n<p> Finally, the effect of delays varies depending on the context and situation. News sites, shopping sites and travel sites are often part of different kinds of user journeys, and the entire curve above might look different for each of them. Even within a context, site design and user behavior can change over time. </p> \n<p> Although this is more difficult than we may have hoped, a distribution of outcomes at different levels of performance still contains useful hints. In particular, the distribution reveals how many users we may lose (or are losing currently) at a given level of performance. In addition, the steepness of the curve at different points can tell you how much return you’ll get for optimising speed by a particular amount. These are important factors in your tradeoff decision, since your time as a designer or engineer is also valuable. </p> \n<p> So instead of looking for a single magic number, our goal is to find in the research useful ranges of values and reasonable guidelines. For example: </p> \n<ul> \n <li> One study found that delays decreased satisfaction and intention to return. On unfamiliar sites, 2 seconds of delay was enough to cause most of the drop – familiar sites bottomed out after longer delays. On unfamiliar sites, task performance also suffered, with most of the drop observed with delays of up to 4 seconds.<sup style=\"font-size: x-small;\"><a href=\"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html#f3\">3</a></sup> </li> \n <li> Another study involved navigating a nested menu on a web page. A range of delays, 3 seconds apart, was tested for loading each panel. Satisfaction dropped when increasing the delay from 0 to 3 seconds and again when going from 9 to 12 seconds. Intention to return also dropped with the 12-second delay. A 6-second delay was enough for some participants to remark on the site being slow.<sup style=\"font-size: x-small;\"><a href=\"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html#f4\">4</a></sup> </li> \n <li> One study found that mobile web users didn’t tend to keep their attention on the screen for more than 4–8 seconds at a time.<sup style=\"font-size: x-small;\"><a href=\"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html#f5\">5</a></sup> This would mean that if they avert their attention before your page has loaded, the time they’re looking away further delays how soon they finally see the page. So a 5-second load time might turn into a 10-second effective delay. </li> \n <li> It’s been suggested that the speed of a system’s response should be comparable to the delays humans experience when they interact with one another. This has led to guidance that responses should take about 1–4 seconds.<sup style=\"font-size: x-small;\"><a href=\"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html#f6\">6</a></sup> </li>\n</ul> \n<p> The empirical studies are drawn from data with high variability and gradual drop-offs rather than hard thresholds, and the others are based on predictions from theory. But collectively they suggest that it’s worth aiming to keep load times within a couple of seconds. </p> \n<p> The <a href=\"https://web.dev/lcp/\">Largest Contentful Paint</a> (LCP) metric measures when a page-to-page navigation appears complete to a user. We <a href=\"https://web.dev/lcp/#what-is-a-good-lcp-score\">recommend</a> sites aim to <strong>keep LCP under 2.5 seconds for 75% of their page loads</strong>. This recommendation is further informed by Chrome analysis of the web today and aims to be feasible for enough sites to attain in practice. For more details of that analysis, see <a href=\"https://web.dev/defining-core-web-vitals-thresholds/\">Defining the Core Web Vitals metric thresholds</a>. </p> \n<h2 style=\"font-size: 2em; line-height: 1.3em; margin: 20px 0px 10px;\">Interruptions and errors from instability</h2> \n<p> Most web pages need to load several elements, and often these load progressively. This can actually be a good thing: if some content appears as early as possible, it may allow a user to start making progress towards their goal without waiting for everything to load. </p> \n<p> However, if the position of already-visible elements shifts as others load, this can negatively affect the user’s experience in two ways. </p> \n<p> One is that if an element they’re looking at suddenly moves, it will take their eyes at least a couple hundred milliseconds to find its new position.<sup style=\"font-size: x-small;\"><a href=\"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html#f7\">7</a></sup> If it scrolled out of view, it will take much longer. This type of interruption slows the user journey and can be very frustrating. </p> \n<p> A more serious consequence is that unexpected layout shifts can lead to errors. If the user is trying to tap an element that then moves, they may end up tapping something else that moved into its original position. This is because the delay from perceiving the shift, deciding to abandon their action and then doing so can make it <em>impossible</em> for a human to respond appropriately. This could mean clicking a link or ad or \"Buy Now\" button unintentionally and significantly disrupting the user's intended journey. </p> \n<p> <a href=\"https://web.dev/cls/\">Cumulative Layout Shift</a> (CLS) measures how frequent and severe unexpected layout shifts are on a page. Fewer shifts mean less chance for interruption and errors. We <a href=\"https://web.dev/cls/#what-is-a-good-cls-score\">recommend</a> sites <strong>aim for a CLS of less than 0.1 for 75% of page loads</strong>. </p> \n<h2 style=\"font-size: 2em; line-height: 1.3em; margin: 20px 0px 10px;\">Distraction and errors from low responsiveness</h2> \n<p> While page loads represent the larger transitions in a user’s journey – like entering a building – the small steps also matter. </p> \n<p> When you’re walking, you don’t really want to be conscious of the mechanics of walking. Ideally, you actually forget that you’re walking and can focus on other things, like finding your way. But something like having a stone in your shoe will interfere with that concentration. </p> \n<p> Likewise, you don’t want users’ experience to suffer from frictions in their moment-to-moment interactions with your site. Here are some research insights relevant to achieving this: </p> \n<ul> \n <li> One study found that a delay in visual feedback from touch screen buttons became perceivable when it was increased from 70ms to 100ms. When it was further increased from 100ms to 150ms, people also rated the quality of the buttons as significantly lower.<sup style=\"font-size: x-small;\"><a href=\"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html#f8\">8</a></sup> </li> \n <li> One experiment showed that in an animation, the illusion that one event caused another started breaking when there was a delay of about 100ms.<sup style=\"font-size: x-small;\"><a href=\"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html#f9\">9</a></sup> It’s been suggested that similarly, the illusion of direct manipulation in user interfaces will break down with delays longer than this.<sup style=\"font-size: x-small;\"><a href=\"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html#f10\">10</a></sup> (This guidance was apparently also informed by an earlier best-guess recommendation that actions should have a visible effect within 100–200ms.<sup style=\"font-size: x-small;\"><a href=\"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html#f11\">11</a></sup>) </li>\n</ul> \n<p> Just as for LCP, there’s no “magic number”, only markers representing distributions. Some individuals are much more sensitive than others,<sup style=\"font-size: x-small;\"><a href=\"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html#f12\">12</a></sup> and shorter delays may be noticed when haptic or auditory feedback is involved.<sup style=\"font-size: x-small;\"><a href=\"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/05/the-science-behind-web-vitals.html#f13\">13</a></sup></p> \n<p> Aside from changing how the UI feels, delaying something people expect to be near-instantaneous can lead them to make errors. They may repeat an action because they think it didn’t work, and the second action can have an undesirable effect. For example, they may click an “add to cart” button twice and not realise that they’re now buying two items. </p> \n<p> The responsiveness related to these experiences is measured by <a href=\"https://web.dev/fid/\">First Input Delay</a> (FID), and we <a href=\"https://web.dev/fid/#what-is-a-good-fid-score\">recommend</a> sites aim to <strong>keep FID under 100 milliseconds for 75% of page loads</strong>. </p> \n<h2 style=\"font-size: 2em; line-height: 1.3em; margin: 20px 0px 10px;\">Impact</h2> \n<p> We analyzed millions of page impressions to understand how these metrics and thresholds affect users. We found that when a site meets the above thresholds, <strong>users are 24% less likely to abandon page loads</strong> (by leaving the page before any content has been painted). </p> \n<p> We also looked specifically at news and shopping sites, sites whose businesses depend on traffic and task completion, and found similar numbers: <strong>22% less abandonment for news sites</strong> and <strong>24% less abandonment for shopping sites</strong>. There are few interventions that can show this level of improvement for online businesses, and results like these are part of the reason we and our ecosystem partners prioritize the Web Vitals metrics. </p> \n<p> Providing a smooth journey for users is one of the most effective ways to grow online traffic and web-based businesses. We hope the Web Vitals metrics and thresholds will provide publishers, developers and business owners with clear and actionable ways to make their sites part of fast, interruption-free journeys for more users. </p> \n<p> <em> Amar Sagoo, Staff Interaction Designer<br /> Annie Sullivan, Software Engineer<br /> Vivek Sekhar, Product Manager </em></p> \n<hr style=\"margin: 50px auto 20px; width: 50%;\" /> \n<div style=\"font-size: smaller; line-height: 1;\"> \n <sup id=\"f1\" style=\"font-size: x-small;\">1</sup> Miller, R. B. (1968). \n <a href=\"https://scholar.google.com/scholar?cluster=15394303020349681856\">Response time in man-computer conversational transactions</a>. In \n <em>Proceedings of the December 9-11, 1968, fall joint computer conference, part I</em> (pp. 267–277). \n <br /> \n <sup id=\"f2\" style=\"font-size: x-small;\">2</sup> Shneiderman, B. Our primary objectives are to ensure Chrome continues to be stable, secure, and work reliably for anyone who depends on them. We’ll continue to prioritize any updates related to security, which will be included in Chrome 80.</p><br />Please follow the <a href=\"https://chromereleases.googleblog.com/\" rel=\"nofollow\" target=\"_blank\">Chrome Releases blog</a> for updates.<br /><br />Posted by the Chrome Release Team ","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 18 Mar 2020 17:01:00 +0000","feedId":649,"bgimg":"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-vkF7AFJOwBk/VkQxeAGi1mI/AAAAAAAARYo/57denvsQ8zA/s1600-r/logo_chromium.png","linkMd5":"76d5aa286180654c1f05a1a5cceac270","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn61@2020_5/2020/08/16/12-29-50-106_40b5b15e774afa69.webp","metaImg":"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-vkF7AFJOwBk/VkQxeAGi1mI/AAAAAAAARYo/57denvsQ8zA/s1600-r/logo_chromium.png","destWidth":533,"destHeight":537,"author":"Chromium Blog"},{"createdTime":"2020-08-16 20:29:49","updatedTime":"2020-08-16 20:29:49","title":"Chrome 81: Near Field Communications, Augmented Reality, and More","link":"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/02/chrome-81-near-field-communications.html","description":"Unless otherwise noted, changes described below apply to the newest Chrome beta channel release for Android, Chrome OS, Linux, macOS, and Windows. Learn more about the features listed here through the provided links or from the list on <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/features#milestone%3D81\">ChromeStatus.com</a>. Chrome 81 is beta as of February 13, 2020.<br /><h1>Web NFC for mobile</h1>NFC stands for Near Field Communications, a short-range wireless technology for transmitting small amounts of data, usually between a specialized NFC device and a reader. If you've scanned a badge to enter a building, you may have used used NFC. <br /><br />Web NFC allows a web app to read and write to NFC tags. This opens new use cases to the web, including providing information about museum exhibits, inventory management, providing information in a conference badge, and many others. <br /><br /><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-29969d32-7fff-be1d-2f9d-3e21246182f1\"><span style=\"font-family: arial; font-size: 11pt; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\"><span style=\"border: none; display: inline-block; height: 352px; overflow: hidden; width: 624px;\"><img height=\"352\" src=\"https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/7gAWZBHaZVtx4S0xBexbXxCVH726FasA-IwRi8TAoV77RTk6PrPKXZ4lDbhC5Wa1x3h8oFOF9Pv83n-MH4PZKGc6_oArJl_tPOOgN80vQQd1YJIHXeg3QykWGAbpbCnhjAwBrOtQ\" style=\"margin-left: 0px; margin-top: 0px;\" width=\"624\" /></span></span></span><em>A demonstration of Web NFC cards</em><br /><em><br /></em>Reading and writing are simple operations. You'll need a little instruction for constructing and interpreting payloads, but it's not complicated. Fortunately, we have an article, <a href=\"https://www.google.com/url?q=https://web.dev/nfc&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1581287505442000&amp;usg=AFQjCNG4PP2zLimwjoHBWgA7SoDnwuJteA\">Interact with NFC devices on the web</a>. Check it out. A few code samples are shown below.<br /><br />Writing a string to an NFC tag:<br /><br /><pre>if (\"NDEFWriter\" in window) {<br />  const writer = new NDEFWriter();<br />  await writer.write(\"Hello world!\");<br />}<br /></pre><br />Scanning messages from NFC tags:<br /><br /><pre>if (\"NDEFReader\" in window) {<br />  const reader = new NDEFReader();<br />  await reader.scan();<br />  reader.onreading = ({ message }) =&gt; {<br />    console.log(`Message read from a NFC tag: ${message}`);<br />  };<br />}<br /></pre><br />Chrome 81 introduces the mobile web to NFC with an origin trial. See the Origin Trials section for information on signing up and for a list of other origin trials starting in this release.<br /><h1>Augmented Reality and Hit Testing</h1><div class=\"separator\" style=\"clear: both; text-align: center;\"><a href=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-pR2AXHNN6E8/XkXMqlCKdEI/AAAAAAAABGI/ZUcAoMudEiceorz1Kco8ChCeWkFZXs87QCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/augmented-reality.png\" style=\"clear: right; float: right; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: 1em;\"><img border=\"0\" data-original-height=\"1600\" data-original-width=\"900\" height=\"400\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-pR2AXHNN6E8/XkXMqlCKdEI/AAAAAAAABGI/ZUcAoMudEiceorz1Kco8ChCeWkFZXs87QCLcBGAsYHQ/s400/augmented-reality.png\" width=\"225\" /></a></div>Chrome 81 adds two new immersive features to the web, both designed to support augmented reality. The WebXR Device API, first enabled in Chrome 79, now supports augmented reality. We've also added support for the WebXR Hit Test API, an API for placing objects in a real-world view. <br /><br />If you've already used the new API to create virtual reality, you'll be happy to know there's very little new to learn to use AR. This is because the spec was designed with the spectrum of immersive experiences in mind. Regardless of the degree of augmentation or virtualization, the application flow is the same. The differences are merely a matter of setting and requesting different properties during object creation.<br /><div class=\"separator\" style=\"clear: both; text-align: center;\"><a href=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-gEG39-j_4Us/XkXNVIFAgPI/AAAAAAAABGQ/0RTAGN2piY4dbckx9zO0aAuZn2Ta0uVJwCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/reticales.png\" style=\"clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;\"><img border=\"0\" data-original-height=\"1600\" data-original-width=\"900\" height=\"400\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-gEG39-j_4Us/XkXNVIFAgPI/AAAAAAAABGQ/0RTAGN2piY4dbckx9zO0aAuZn2Ta0uVJwCLcBGAsYHQ/s400/reticales.png\" width=\"225\" /></a></div><br />The WebXR Hit Test API provides a means for an immersive experience to interact with the real world. Specifically, it enables you to place virtual objects on real-world points in a camera view. The image below from one of the Immersive Web Working Group's sample apps illustrates this. The broken blue circle indicates a point returned from the hit test API. If I tap the screen a sunflower will be placed there. The new API captures both the location of a hit test and the orientation of the point that was detected. You'll notice in the image a sunflower has been placed on both the floor and the wall. <br /><br />If you're completely new to the WebXR Device API, check out our earlier articles, <a href=\"https://web.dev/vr-comes-to-the-web/\">Virtual reality comes to the web</a> and Virtual reality comes to the web, part II. If you're already familiar with entering a WebXR session and constructing a frame loop, then check out <a href=\"https://web.dev/web-ar/\" target=\"_blank\">our new article on Web AR</a>. Also check out our article on the <a href=\"https://web.dev/ar-hit-test/\" target=\"_blank\">WebXR Hit Test API</a>.<br /><h1>Origin Trials</h1>This version of Chrome introduces the origin trials described below. Origin trials allow you to try new features and give feedback on usability, practicality, and effectiveness to the web standards community. To register for any of the origin trials currently supported in Chrome, including the ones described below, visit the <a href=\"https://developers.chrome.com/origintrials/#/trials/active\">Origin Trials dashboard</a>. To learn more about origin trials themselves, visit the <a href=\"https://github.com/GoogleChrome/OriginTrials/blob/gh-pages/developer-guide.md\">Origin Trials Guide for Web Developers</a>.<br /><h2>PointerLock unadjustedMovement</h2>Scripts now have the ability to <a href=\"https://developers.chrome.com/origintrials/#/view_trial/-8986933056417824767\">request unadjusted and unaccelerated mouse movement data</a> when in PointerLock. If <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">unadjustedMovement</span> is&nbsp;set to <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">true</span>, then pointer movements will not be affected by the underlying platform modifications such as mouse acceleration.<br /><h1>Other features in this release</h1><h2>Buffered Flag for Long Tasks</h2>Chrome 81 <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/feature/6306761277440000\">updates the buffered flag of <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">PerformanceObserver</span></a> to support long tasks. In particular, this feature provides a way to gain insight into early long tasks for apps or pages that register a <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">PerformanceObserver</span> early.<br /><h2>CSS image-orientation property</h2>Chrome will by default respect EXIF metadata within images indicating desired orientation. The accompanying <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">image-orientation</span> property allows developers to override this behavior.<br /><h2>CSS Color Adjust: color-scheme</h2>A <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/features/6070987093180416\">new meta tag and </a>CSS property lets sites opt-in to following the preferred color scheme when rendering UI elements such as default colors of form controls and scrollbars as well as the used values of the CSS system colors. For Chrome 81 only initial color and background are affected.<br /><h2>Exclude Implicit Tracks from grid-template-rows and grid-template-columns Resolved Values</h2><a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/feature/5958076270116864\">Implicit tracks are now excluded from the resolved values</a> of the <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">grid-template-rows</span> and <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">grid-template-columns</span>. Previously, all tracks were included, whether implicit or explicit.<div><br /></div><div>Note: This was mistakenly included though it did not actually ship.<br /><h2>hrefTranslate attribute on HTMLAnchorElement</h2>The <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">HTMLAnchorElement</span> <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/features/6298864638230528\">now has an <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">hrefTranslate</span> attribute</a>, providing the ability for a page to hint to a user agent's translation engine that the destination site of an href should be translated if followed.<br /><h2>IntersectionObserver Document Root</h2>The <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">IntersectionObserver()</span> constructor <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/features/5714474851893248\">now takes a <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">Document</span> as the 'root' argument</a>, causing intersections to be calculated against the scrolling viewport of the document. This is primarily targeted towards observers running in an iframe. Previously, there was no way to measure intersection with the scrolling viewport of the iframe's document.<br /><h2>Modernized Form Controls</h2>In version 81, Chrome modernizes the appearance of form controls on Windows, ChromeOS, and Linux while improving their accessibility and touch support. (Mac and Android support are coming soon.) It's hoped that this will reduce the need to build custom form controls. This change is the result of collaboration between Microsoft and Google. For more information, see the <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZFvPLrKZywA\">recent talk at CDS</a> or the <a href=\"https://blogs.windows.com/msedgedev/2019/10/15/form-controls-microsoft-edge-chromium/\">MS blog post</a>. For a closer look at the controls, <a href=\"http://concrete-hardboard.glitch.me/\">this page</a> gives an example of all of the elements that changed.<br /><h2>Move onwebkit{animation,transition}XX handlers to GlobalEventHandlers</h2>Until now, the prefixed <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">onwebkit{animation,transition}XX</span> handlers were only available on the Window object in Chrome. They are <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/feature/4833112531927040\">now on <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">HTMLElement</span> and <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">Document</span></a> as required by the spec. This fix brings Chrome in line with Gecko and Webkit.<br /><br />Note: This change is intended to improve interoperability on existing web pages. These handlers are still obsolete so web developers should use the non-refixed versions on new pages.<br /><h2>Position State for Media Session</h2>Adds <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/feature/5677548182700032\">support for tracking position state</a> in a media session. The position state is a combination of the playback rate, duration, and current playback time. This can then be used by browsers to display position in the UI and with the addition of seeking can support seeking/scrubbing too. For a code sample and demonstration, see <a href=\"https://googlechrome.github.io/samples/media-session/video.html\">our sample</a>.<br /><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-cbd0a62b-7fff-bb9d-4e25-e7fde1f69049\"><span style=\"font-family: arial; font-size: 11pt; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\"><span style=\"border: none; display: inline-block; height: 345px; overflow: hidden; width: 471px;\"><img height=\"345\" src=\"https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/D6iCSy9rZ2_aKbEc1w90p97Vh_nRkBOQayDdKLSMyOp82uP8kgzHgHku9Wvvism3glhED9FLUsCAJecp0EwDERRbSGmEUIbevOx9V0x8VNLXofO4jFBdF_UMdhAPydC-v_-4lDYR\" style=\"margin-left: 0px; margin-top: 0px;\" width=\"471\" /></span></span></span><br /><h2>SubmitEvent</h2>Chrome now supports <a href=\"https://chromestatus.com/features/5187248926490624\">a <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">SubmitEvent</span> type</a>, an <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">Event</span> subtype which is dispatched on form submission. The <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">SubmitEvent</span> has a submitter property that refers to attributes of the submitter button including the entry data, the <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">formaction</span> attribute, the <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">formenctype</span> attribute, the <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">formmethod</span> attribute, and the <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">formtarget</span> attribute. <br /><br />Currently, applications are doing their own form submission by calling <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">preventDefault()</span> during <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">onsubmit</span>. This approach has the limitation that the received event does not include the button that triggered the submission.<br /><h2>WebAudio: ConvolverNode.channelCount and channelCountMode</h2>For a <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">ConvolverNode</span>, the channelCount <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/feature/6248507407073280\">can now be set to 1 or 2</a>. The <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">channelCountMode</span> can be <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">\"explicit\"</span> or <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">\"clamped-max\"</span>. Previously, a <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">channelCount</span> of 1 was not allowed and neither was a mode of <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">\"explicit\"</span>.<br /><br />This release also extends <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">ConvolverNode</span> capabilities slightly to allow developers to choose the desired behavior without having to add a <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">GainNode</span> to do the desired mixing.<br /><h2>WebRTC</h2><h3>RTCPeerConnection.onicecandidateerror event changes</h3>The <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">candidateerror</span> event <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/feature/5698302152540160\">now has an explicit address and port</a>, replacing <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">hostCandidate</span>.<br /><h3>onclosing Event for RTCDataChannel</h3><a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/feature/5733328181264384\">Adds the <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">onclosing</span> event to the <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">RTCDataChannel</span> object</a>, which signals to the user of a data channel that the other side has started closing the channel. The user agent will continue reading from the queue (if it contains anything) until the queue is empty, but no more data can be sent.<br /><h2>WorkerOptions for shared workers constructor   </h2><a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/features/5642256117661696\">Adds the <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">WorkerOptions</span> object</a> as the second argument for a shared worker constructor. The previous second argument, a string containing the worker's name is still supported.<br /><h2>WritableStream.close()</h2><span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">WritableStream</span> objects <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/feature/5440098147500032\">now have a <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">close()</span> method</a> that closes a stream if it is unlocked. This is directly equivalent to getting a writer, using the writer to close the stream, and then unlocking it again.<br /><h1>JavaScript</h1>This version of Chrome incorporates version 8.1 of the V8 JavaScript engine. It specifically includes the changes listed below. You can find a complete <a href=\"https://v8.dev/blog\">list of recent changes</a> in the V8 release notes.<br /><h2>Intl.DisplayNames()</h2>The <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">Intl.DisplayNames()</span> object lets an app or script <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/feature/4965112605573120\">get localized names of language, script, currency codes, and commonly used names of date fields and symbols.</a> This will reduce the size of apps (thereby improving latency), make it easier to build internationalized UI components, reduce translation costs, and provide more consistent translations across the web.<br /><h1>Deprecations, and Removals</h1>This version of Chrome introduces the deprecations and removals listed below. Visit ChromeStatus.com for lists of <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/features#browsers.chrome.status%3A%22Deprecated%22\">current deprecations</a>  and <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/features#browsers.chrome.status:%22Removed%22\">previous removals</a>.<br /><h2>Deprecation and Remove \"basic-card\" support Payment Handler</h2>This version of Chrome removes the basic-card polyfill for Payment Request API in iOS Chrome. As a result, the Payment Request API is temporarily disabled in iOS Chrome. For full details, see <a href=\"https://www.google.com/url?q=https://blog.chromium.org/2020/01/rethinking-payment-request-for-ios.html&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1581287505507000&amp;usg=AFQjCNFf5bi-68IcQTns9C24XVn_BTwgGw\">Rethinking Payment Request for iOS</a>.<br /><h2>Remove supportedType field from BasicCardRequest</h2>Specifying <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">\"supportedTypes\":[type]</span> parameter for <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">\"basic-card\"</span> payment method shows cards of only the requested type, which is one of <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">\"credit\", \"debit\",</span> or <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">\"prepaid\"</span>.<br /><br />The card type parameter has been removed from the spec and is <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/feature/5709702556024832\">now removed from Chrome</a>, because of the difficulty of accurate card type determination. Merchants today must check card type with their PSP, because they cannot trust the card type filter in the browser:<br /><br /><ul><li>Only issuing banks know the card type with certainty and downloadable card type databases have low accuracy, so it's impossible to know accurately the type of the cards stored locally in the browser.</li><li>The <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">\"basic-card\"</span> payment method in Chrome no longer shows cards from Google Pay, which may have connections with issuing banks.</li></ul><br />Firefox removed <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">\"supportedTypes\"</span> in version 65.<br /><h2>Remove the &lt;discard&gt; element</h2>Chrome 81 <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/features/4870172764536832\">removes the <span style=\"font-family: &quot;courier new&quot;, courier, monospace;\">&lt;discard&gt;</span> element</a>. It is only implemented in Chromium, and is thus not possible to use interoperably. For most use cases it can be replaced with a combination of animation of the 'display' property and a removal (JavaScript) callback/event handler.<br /><h2>Remove TLS 1.0 and TLS 1.1</h2><div><b>Note:</b>&nbsp;Removal of TLS 1.0 and TLS 1.1 has been delayed to Chrome 83, which is expected to ship in late May 2020.</div><div><br /></div>This version of Chrome <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/feature/5759116003770368\">removes TLS 1.0 and TLS 1.1</a>. TLS (Transport Layer Security) is the protocol which secures HTTPS. It has a long history stretching back to the nearly twenty-year-old TLS 1.0 and its even older predecessor, SSL. Both TLS 1.0 and 1.1 have a number of weaknesses.<br /><ul><li>TLS 1.0 and 1.1 use MD5 and SHA-1, both weak hashes, in the transcript hash for the Finished message.</li><li>TLS 1.0 and 1.1 use MD5 and SHA-1 in the server signature. (Note: this is not the signature in the certificate.)</li><li>TLS 1.0 and 1.1 only support RC4 and CBC ciphers. RC4 is broken and has since been removed. TLS's CBC mode construction is flawed and was vulnerable to attacks.</li><li>TLS 1.0's CBC ciphers additionally construct their initialization vectors incorrectly.</li><li>TLS 1.0 is no longer PCI-DSS compliant.</li></ul>Supporting TLS 1.2 is a prerequisite to avoiding the above problems. The TLS working group has deprecated TLS 1.0 and 1.1. Chrome deprecated these features in version 72 in early 2019.<br /><h2>TLS 1.3 downgrade hardening bypass</h2>TLS 1.3 includes a backwards-compatible hardening measure <a href=\"https://www.chromestatus.com/feature/5128354539765760\">to strengthen downgrade protections</a>. However, when we shipped TLS 1.3 last year, we had to partially disable this measure due to incompatibilities with some non-compliant TLS-terminating proxies. Chrome currently implements the hardening measure for certificates which chain up to known roots, but allows a bypass for certificates chaining up to unknown roots. We intend to enable it for all connections.<br /><br />Downgrade protection mitigates the security impact of the various legacy options we retain for compatibility. This means user's connections are more secure and, when security vulnerabilities are discovered, it is less of a scramble to respond to them. (That, in turn, means fewer broken sites for users down the road.) This also aligns with <a href=\"https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc8446\">RFC 8446</a>.</div>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 14 Feb 2020 01:55:00 +0000","feedId":649,"bgimg":"https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/7gAWZBHaZVtx4S0xBexbXxCVH726FasA-IwRi8TAoV77RTk6PrPKXZ4lDbhC5Wa1x3h8oFOF9Pv83n-MH4PZKGc6_oArJl_tPOOgN80vQQd1YJIHXeg3QykWGAbpbCnhjAwBrOtQ","linkMd5":"86d1426116241dc0eb995ca8e7f0987a","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn22@2020_4/2020/08/16/12-29-54-871_642cc4704a3c47ea.webp","destWidth":600,"destHeight":338,"author":"Chromium Blog","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/7gAWZBHaZVtx4S0xBexbXxCVH726FasA-IwRi8TAoV77RTk6PrPKXZ4lDbhC5Wa1x3h8oFOF9Pv83n-MH4PZKGc6_oArJl_tPOOgN80vQQd1YJIHXeg3QykWGAbpbCnhjAwBrOtQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn74@2020_3/2020/08/16/12-29-55-349_642cc4704a3c47ea.webp","https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-pR2AXHNN6E8/XkXMqlCKdEI/AAAAAAAABGI/ZUcAoMudEiceorz1Kco8ChCeWkFZXs87QCLcBGAsYHQ/s400/augmented-reality.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn9@2020_6/2020/08/16/12-29-50-097_539049b94ca0fac0.webp","https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-gEG39-j_4Us/XkXNVIFAgPI/AAAAAAAABGQ/0RTAGN2piY4dbckx9zO0aAuZn2Ta0uVJwCLcBGAsYHQ/s400/reticales.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn82@2020_5/2020/08/16/12-29-51-310_c6187ea5904f7af1.webp","https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/D6iCSy9rZ2_aKbEc1w90p97Vh_nRkBOQayDdKLSMyOp82uP8kgzHgHku9Wvvism3glhED9FLUsCAJecp0EwDERRbSGmEUIbevOx9V0x8VNLXofO4jFBdF_UMdhAPydC-v_-4lDYR":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn26@2020_3/2020/08/16/12-29-51-599_267b7c14c2b3db1d.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-08-16 20:29:49","updatedTime":"2020-08-16 20:29:49","title":"More secure and convenient Autofill coming to Chrome","link":"http://blog.chromium.org/2020/07/more-secure-and-convenient-autofill.html","description":"<div class=\"separator\"><br /></div>Chrome can help you fill in forms securely - everywhere on the web. Recently, following the <a href=\"https://www.betterads.org/standards/\" target=\"_blank\">Better Ads Standards</a>, we have taken steps to address ads that most people find unacceptable. Prior to that, we also launched a set of protections against <a href=\"https://blog.chromium.org/2018/11/further-protections-from-harmful-ad.html\" target=\"_blank\">abusive experiences</a> in Chrome.<br /><div><br /></div><div>We have recently discovered that a fraction of a percent of ads consume a disproportionate share of device resources, such as battery and network data, without the user knowing about it. These ads (such as those that mine cryptocurrency, are poorly programmed, or are unoptimized for network usage) can drain battery life, saturate already strained networks, and cost money.&nbsp;</div><div><br /></div><div>In order to save our users’ batteries and data plans, and provide them with a good experience on the web, Chrome will limit the resources a display ad can use before the user interacts with the ad. When an ad reaches its limit, the ad's frame will navigate to an error page, informing the user that the ad has used too many resources. Here is an example of an ad that has been unloaded:</div><div><br /></div><div style=\"text-align: center;\"><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-95fa81a8-7fff-2aba-83ac-a917a7a00ac3\"><span style=\"color: #5f6368; font-family: Roboto, sans-serif; font-size: 10pt; font-variant-east-asian: normal; font-variant-numeric: normal; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\"><span style=\"border: 1pt solid #f3f3f3; display: inline-block; height: 453px; overflow: hidden; width: 234px;\"><img height=\"470.0000000000001\" src=\"https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/urkFgM0cxX7_D04eaYtcy5_4tfKorlegizrsFFsjhIfiyyW6BYWcjqENe7_RVAvHlQ4GCEccz19eVCkIf1Jr_z5kq4WKLwr5QM6oV-53kl4QNBLvO4qyQT5nn3JAeNePaywncS2X\" style=\"margin-left: 0px; margin-top: -17px;\" width=\"234\" /></span></span></span></div><div><br /></div><div>To determine the threshold limits for the unloading, we extensively measured the ads Chrome sees. We targeted the most egregious ads, those that use more CPU or network bandwidth than 99.9% of all detected ads for that resource. Chrome is setting the thresholds to 4MB of network data or 15 seconds of CPU usage in any 30 second period, or 60 seconds of total CPU usage. While only 0.3% of ads exceed this threshold today, they account for 27% of network data used by ads and 28% of all ad CPU usage.<br /><br /></div><div><br /></div><div style=\"text-align: center;\"><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-240b3b9c-7fff-48f4-558b-5d6716f07fa4\"><span style=\"font-family: &quot;google sans&quot; , sans-serif; font-size: 12pt; font-weight: 700; vertical-align: baseline; white-space: pre-wrap;\"><span style=\"border: none; display: inline-block; height: 356px; overflow: hidden; width: 467px;\"><img height=\"356\" src=\"https://lh3.googleusercontent.com/2FruoINcpABRGu0WRr7NyQVegnGQxADVJwYCueTT9N8CXvC6mSiZFUDd1bS5uJbSC9Xz4TTIsc78XPjDPmJziycwnYF-kvlh0rZzRQA60UYo8oPi_SVi4g62OrF3x7xpVzsT-Dw5\" style=\"margin-left: 0px; margin-top: 0px;\" title=\"Heavy Ads Resource Usage\" width=\"467\" /></span></span></span></div><div style=\"text-align: center;\"><br /></div><div><br /><div style=\"text-align: center;\"><span style=\"font-size: x-small;\">The overall percentage of heavy and non-heavy ads and the total resource usage of each</span></div><div style=\"text-align: center;\"><br /></div></div><div>We intend to experiment with this over the next several months, and to launch this intervention on Chrome stable near the end of August. Our intent with this extended rollout is to give appropriate time for ad creators and tool providers to prepare and incorporate these thresholds into their workflows. +0000","feedId":649,"bgimg":"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-vkF7AFJOwBk/VkQxeAGi1mI/AAAAAAAARYo/57denvsQ8zA/s1600-r/logo_chromium.png","linkMd5":"cafeb304e5efbec7375629f1a70e63eb","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn61@2020_5/2020/08/16/12-29-50-106_40b5b15e774afa69.webp","metaImg":"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-vkF7AFJOwBk/VkQxeAGi1mI/AAAAAAAARYo/57denvsQ8zA/s1600-r/logo_chromium.png","destWidth":533,"destHeight":537,"author":"Chromium
